    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<a href="/questions/829939-does-anyone-know-if-this-quote-is-actually">Does anyone know if this quote is actually by A.A. Milne (rather than Disney), and if so, spoken by which character? &quot;As soon as I saw you, I knew an adventure was going to happen.&quot; Wording may be slightly different.</a>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">There are two similar quotes of Winnie-the-Pooh as written by Milne;<br/><br/>“When you see someone putting on his Big Boots, you can be pretty sure that an Adv<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">There are two similar quotes of Winnie-the-Pooh as written by Milne;<br /><br />“When you see someone putting on his Big Boots, you can be pretty sure that an Adventure is going to happen.”<br /><br />“As soon as I saw you, I knew a grand adventure was about to happen.” <br /><a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2433141756?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70395042-fergus?shelf=books-for-children">books-for-children</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2433141756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15659606716218972363">Back in the fifties, one Christmas, I had a mammoth case of the flu. But somehow for a little kid the world is always a place of wonder, no matter HOW bad he feels...<br/><br/>I remember reading the story of the Flying Dutchman in bed on Christmas Eve - with an incipient fever blackening the edges of my reverie, like the ominous apparition of a major typhoon darkening the edges of the sea.<br/><br/>So that, reading this book of the Dutchman’s restless quest became my last clear memory of that Christmas, otherwise s</span>
  <span id="freeText15659606716218972363" style="display:none">Back in the fifties, one Christmas, I had a mammoth case of the flu. But somehow for a little kid the world is always a place of wonder, no matter HOW bad he feels...<br /><br />I remember reading the story of the Flying Dutchman in bed on Christmas Eve - with an incipient fever blackening the edges of my reverie, like the ominous apparition of a major typhoon darkening the edges of the sea.<br /><br />So that, reading this book of the Dutchman’s restless quest became my last clear memory of that Christmas, otherwise shrouded in delirium.<br /><br />The story stayed with me all through my life as the story of an unlucky guy who has to sail the Seven Seas of life to find the girl of his dreams...<br /><br />Because that, as it turned out, became the story of my OWN life, painfully shy kid that I was!<br /><br />But a few days later I was starting to feel better again. <br /><br />My Mom, ever the librarian - with a kind, edifying heart - gave me a book about kids in France supporting the Resistance during the War. <br /><br />Well, there was just no way I would read an ADULT book! <br /><br />As I pulled the covers over me to settle down and read, I was holding my tattered copy of The House at Pooh Corner.<br /><br />Well, I guess the children's aspirin I had been given had psychotropic effects, because as I read once again of Christopher Robin and Pooh's walk into the forest I fell asleep. <br /><br />In my dream the immortal pair were walking through an ENCHANTED forest - full of fairies, princesses and trolls!<br /><br />I LOVED this book. And it was not till much later that I learned the REAL Winnie-the-Pooh was Canadian, like me. <br /><br />I kid you not. <br /><br />The real Winnie, whose name was Winnipeg (like the city), was purchased in North Bay, Ontario, by a kindly veterinarian who had signed up with the Canadian Army in WWI as a horse doctor. <br /><br />Winnipeg became the official mascot of the Second Canadian Infantry Brigade. When the vet was demobilised, he dropped Winnipeg off at the London Zoo before sailing back to Canada.<br /><br />One day soon after, a young father named A.A. Milne took his son Christopher to the zoo, and showed him Winnipeg the Bear.<br /><br />He read and spelled the bear's name to the young kid. But Christopher just squealed out, "Winnie the Pooh"!<br /><br />And "therein", as Rabelais had it, "hangs a Tale"....</span>
  <a data-text-id="15659606716218972363" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1238336984" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1238336984" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1238336984?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780416789003" href="/book/show/982360.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1238336984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6617406616166511372">So beautifully and so simply written (deceptively so) by A.A. Milne and exquisitely illustrated by E.H. Shepard (initially black/white line drawings and later colour washed by Shepard himself). <br/><br/>These are the stories of a boy and his bear, his world and all the wonderful characters that inhabit that world – 100 Acre Wood, his childhood and ultimately the passing of that childhood. <br/><br/>What could have been (especially considering the era in which they were written) a particularly twee, sickly sweet an</span>
  <span id="freeText6617406616166511372" style="display:none">So beautifully and so simply written (deceptively so) by A.A. Milne and exquisitely illustrated by E.H. Shepard (initially black/white line drawings and later colour washed by Shepard himself). <br /><br />These are the stories of a boy and his bear, his world and all the wonderful characters that inhabit that world – 100 Acre Wood, his childhood and ultimately the passing of that childhood. <br /><br />What could have been (especially considering the era in which they were written) a particularly twee, sickly sweet and very dated collection of stories of childhood, is as about as far from that as it could possibly be. Both collections have certainly stood the test of time extremely well. <br /><br />At the heart of A.A. Milne’s wonderful collections: ‘Winnie-the-Pooh’ and ‘The House at Pooh Corner’ – is of course Pooh, who is such a wonderfully created character – a ‘bear of very little brain’, but a bear who is undeniably wise, funny, loyal, paradoxically clever, who does many brave and wonderful things; a bear who makes mistakes and gets things wrong, but is always forgiven; a bear who is both selfish and greedy (see Honey) and yet kind and thoughtful; a bear who above all else (and clichéd though it may be) lovable. <br /><br />Let us not forget though the lovely cast of supporting characters, including the timid and excitable Piglet, wise (although not) Owl, morose and self-pitying Eeyore, Kango, Roo, Rabbit (and friends and relations) and many others. In these characters, we see ourselves, we all know and Eeyore, the same as we all know a Rabbit – we are all in there somewhere, in some shape or form.<br /><br />A.A. Milnes Winnie-the-Pooh stories are just so simply and so well written, both collections are timeless classics – from the opening lines to the closing ones from the profoundly moving last chapter:<br /><br />“…So, they went off together. But wherever they go, and whatever happens to them on the way, in that enchanted place on the top of the Forest, a little boy and his Bear will always be playing”<br /><br />There is so much more I could write about these books, but it doesn’t feel that I am really conveying quite how wonderful these stories are, I am not doing them justice – what A.A. Milne along with E.H. Shepard have given us is something very special. Both of these collections are classics in every sense of the word. <br /><br />Not to mention the funny, moving and sometime life affirming quotes from ‘Winnie-the-Pooh’<br /><br />“Sometimes the smallest things take up the most room in your heart.”<br />“Rivers know this: there is no hurry. We shall get there some day.”<br />“If the person you are talking to doesn't appear to be listening, be patient. It may simply be that he has a small piece of fluff in his ear.”<br /><br />In summation – just read them.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6617406616166511372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2233555200014008254" style="display:none"><b>Winnie the Pooh and the Cocktail Party</b><br /><br />"Hi. I'm Vikki. Sorry, what did you say your name was? So noisy in here you can't hear a thing. Ed. Got it. Nice to meet you, Ed! What? Oh, I'm a model. Glamor. Thanks! Well, if you've walked past the men's magazine section this week, then you will have. Front cover of Loaded. Really? Hey, that's sweet. No, I mean it. You're really nice. Oh, alright then. Would you believe it, stockings and a honey-pot. That was it. Yes, I do actually. Love it. You're the first person I've ever met who understands. You what?? You actually got your head stuck in it?! Ha! Oh, you are totally cracking me up, I <i>love</i> this story! Ha! Ha! I'm sorry about your friend though, I hope he recovered. It's funny, I just feel so relaxed with you. It's like I've known you for ages. Since I was a little girl in fact. Oh, I see everyone's starting to leave. Look, I know this sounds crazy, like we've only just met and I'll probably regret it in the morning, but come home with me. Um, well, yes and no. I just... I just want to hold you. All night. You do? You really do understand? Ed, I can't believe this. It feels like fate. I'm so happy I met you. OK, let me get my coat. We're outta here."<br /><br />___________________________________________<br /><br />If you want to examine the magazine cover which inspired the review, note that, as the review suggests, it's in dubious taste, and features a mostly naked woman. If that kind of thing offends you, don't look at it! Otherwise, it's at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://img.thesun.co.uk/multimedia/archive/00771/Loaded181-April_180_771021a.jpg">http://img.thesun.co.uk/multimedia/ar...</a><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2233555200014008254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2387205437" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2387205437" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2387205437?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=audible">audible</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=fauna">fauna</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=magic">magic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2018-read">2018-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=series">series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2387205437" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16822670494911230106">As a small child, Winnie the Pooh stories were read to me. As an adult, I read them to my kids and took them to see where Christopher Robin and his childhood friends had lived—the Milnes’ country home at Cotchford Farm, Hartfield in East Sussex, England. We walked in the “Hundred Acre Wood” where he had walked. In real life it is part of Ashdown Forest. We saw the bridge and tumbling creek where sticks had been thrown in on one side and eagerly awaited on the other. I wanted to share with my chi</span>
  <span id="freeText16822670494911230106" style="display:none">As a small child, Winnie the Pooh stories were read to me. As an adult, I read them to my kids and took them to see where Christopher Robin and his childhood friends had lived—the Milnes’ country home at Cotchford Farm, Hartfield in East Sussex, England. We walked in the “Hundred Acre Wood” where he had walked. In real life it is part of Ashdown Forest. We saw the bridge and tumbling creek where sticks had been thrown in on one side and eagerly awaited on the other. I wanted to share with my children the magical place that had incited <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/81466.A_A__Milne" title="A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">A.A. Milne</a> to write the Pooh stories. When we made this trip, years ago, there was not a tourist sign to be found! For more detailed information see here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hundred_Acre_Wood">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hundred...</a><br /><br />My journey with Christopher Robin and Pooh and his pals continued. I picked up <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/462924.The_Enchanted_Places" title="The Enchanted Places by Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Enchanted Places</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/935178.The_Path_through_the_Trees" title="The Path through the Trees by Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Path through the Trees</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1208350.Hollow_On_The_Hill" title="Hollow On The Hill by Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Hollow On The Hill</a> written by Milne’s son, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/259584.Christopher_Milne" title="Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Christopher Milne</a>, the very same Christopher Robin of the stories. They are very good. My journey continued; earlier this year I listened to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_" title="Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie-the-Pooh</a> as an audiobook read by Peter Dennis. This is the only audiobook version authorized by A.A. Milne's son. I fell in love with Pooh, Christopher Robin and his gang all over again. Do not choose a different narrator! That audiobook led to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/776407.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner__Winnie_the_Pooh___2_" title="The House at Pooh Corner (Winnie-the-Pooh, #2) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The House at Pooh Corner</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/821003.When_We_Were_Very_Young__Winnie_the_Pooh___3_" title="When We Were Very Young (Winnie-the-Pooh, #3) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">When We Were Very Young</a>, also read by Peter Dennis. <br /><br />Milne’s first two books, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_" title="Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie-the-Pooh</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/776407.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner__Winnie_the_Pooh___2_" title="The House at Pooh Corner (Winnie-the-Pooh, #2) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The House at Pooh Corner</a> belong together. In the first, we are introduced to a magical place and the friends living there. In the second, a few more adventures are added and then goodbyes are said. Why? Because, quite simply, life goes on. Christopher Robin will soon be off to school. Nothing lasts forever, yet who we are now is a product of what has gone before. So, the two books are different—the first is about saying hello, and the second is about saying goodbye. Goodbye to a place. Goodbye to friends. Goodbye to the early years of childhood. <br /><br />The books are different in another way too. By the end of the second book, we have come to know each figure so well that who they are comes to the fore. Each can, in a subtle way, be seen as people of different personality types. I love that although all have become dear to me, each I love in a different way. All are special, but each in their own different way. Tiger is bouncy, sweet-tempered. Piglet is instinctively smart and clever but always unobtrusively. Eeyore is gloomy and sad, the very opposite of Mr. Cheerful. Owl is learned but has clear limitations in his abilities—he can spell Tuesday so you know at least it isn’t Wednesday. Rabbit is the self-important, officious organizer. And Pooh is just sweet, lovable, humble, meditative Pooh--who has very-little-brains and does like this honey. These friends epitomize people <b>YOU</b> know. <br /><br /><br />The first book is fantastic, the second follows at a close run. It does not however reach up to the excellence of the first. The third, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/821003.When_We_Were_Very_Young__Winnie_the_Pooh___3_" title="When We Were Very Young (Winnie-the-Pooh, #3) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">When We Were Very Young</a>, you can just skip! The first is as good for a young child as for an adult. A parent and child reading it together will both have a wonderful time. The second will be more difficult for a child to fully understand. Here, the humor and wisdom of the lines are directed more to the adult; a deeper meaning is evoked about life-stages and personality types that go beyond the comprehension of a young child.<br /><br />A.A. Milne seldom speaks plainly. He hints. He infers. He uses innuendos. Words are left unsaid. One example is in how we are told “Pooh”, in the name “Winnie-the-Pooh” came to be. A.A. Milne and Christopher Robin often walked down to a pond. On that pond there swam a swan. That swan they called Pooh. Somehow, there are no details, the name of the swan got shifted to Christopher Robin’s teddy bear. That the teddy bear was from Harrods and was originally named Edward is not said, nor that “Edward” got changed to “Winnie” after Christopher Robin had visited London Zoo and seen a real, live black bear from Canada called Winnipeg. There is an interesting story about this bear at Wiki: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winnipeg_(bear)">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winnipe...</a><br /><br />In my view, if you wish as an adult to reread <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_" title="Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie-the-Pooh</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/776407.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner__Winnie_the_Pooh___2_" title="The House at Pooh Corner (Winnie-the-Pooh, #2) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The House at Pooh Corner</a>, the audiobook read by Peter Dennis is the format to choose. The production is exemplary. Each character has his own voice. That voice is in perfect synchrony with the character’s personality. This further enhances one’s appreciation of the second book. A delightful, melodic, piano trill is repeated between the chapters. The music and the lines are sure to bring tears to your eyes. One minute you will laugh. The next you will cry. <br /><br />Milne’s stories are to be shared; they are not to be put into the hands of a child and told to go and read. They will not be properly understood. Their magic lies in the shared, side-by-side reading experience of an adult and a child together. Rereading, as an adult, they evoke childhood memories, and one marvels at the word play, the humor and life wisdom woven into the lines.<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/776407.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner__Winnie_the_Pooh___2_" title="The House at Pooh Corner (Winnie-the-Pooh, #2) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The House at Pooh Corner</a> is about dear friends, friends who are different and each loved for who they are. It is about how it feels when such a friend leaves. It is a book about growing up. <br /><br /><br /><br /><u>Books by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/81466.A_A__Milne" title="A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">A.A. Milne</a></u>:<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_" title="Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie-the-Pooh</a> 5 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/776407.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner__Winnie_the_Pooh___2_" title="The House at Pooh Corner (Winnie-the-Pooh, #2) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The House at Pooh Corner</a> 4 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/821003.When_We_Were_Very_Young__Winnie_the_Pooh___3_" title="When We Were Very Young (Winnie-the-Pooh, #3) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">When We Were Very Young</a> 1 star <br />Each is reviewed under their respective title, even if talk about one must overlap the others.<br /><br /><u>Books by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/259584.Christopher_Milne" title="Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Christopher Milne</a></u>:<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/462924.The_Enchanted_Places" title="The Enchanted Places by Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Enchanted Places</a> 4 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/935178.The_Path_through_the_Trees" title="The Path through the Trees by Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">The Path through the Trees</a> 4 stars<br />*<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1208350.Hollow_On_The_Hill" title="Hollow On The Hill by Christopher Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Hollow On The Hill</a> 4 stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="16822670494911230106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_228675655" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228675655" />
    <a title="·Karen·" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1652898-karen"><img alt="·Karen·" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1434742215p2/1652898.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228675655?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="·Karen·" class="user" itemprop="url" name="·Karen·" href="/user/show/1652898-karen">·Karen·</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780440437956" href="/book/show/1254539.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228675655" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5064517861515275579"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/277496-celebrity-death-match-review-elimination-tournament?chapter=5' rel='nofollow noopener'>Celebrity Death Match Review</a><br/><br/>Semi-Final Match: Winnie-the Pooh vs. Hamlet<br/><br/>BSI Rep: So, the Hundred Acre Wood Corporation has applied for ISO 9000 certification. Would you like to present your company, in terms of its aims and target market?<br/><br/>100 Acre Wood CEO: Yes, indeed. Thank you for this opportunity. For nearly a century now we have provided children and their parents with one of the most essential tools towards abstract thinking, emotional hygiene, social empathy and creativity. Let me bring th</span>
  <span id="freeText5064517861515275579" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/story/show/277496-celebrity-death-match-review-elimination-tournament?chapter=5" rel="nofollow noopener">Celebrity Death Match Review</a><br /><br />Semi-Final Match: Winnie-the Pooh vs. Hamlet<br /><br />BSI Rep: So, the Hundred Acre Wood Corporation has applied for ISO 9000 certification. Would you like to present your company, in terms of its aims and target market?<br /><br />100 Acre Wood CEO: Yes, indeed. Thank you for this opportunity. For nearly a century now we have provided children and their parents with one of the most essential tools towards abstract thinking, emotional hygiene, social empathy and creativity. Let me bring those wide angle goals into a narrower focus.<br /><br />First, abstract thinking. A central idea in education is to enable us to abstract ourselves from the immediate and literal, to form mental models of the world in which we are able to run 'what-if' scenarios. Our thoughts can be guided by something other than what we experience immediately, for instance by words. The imagination is needed to be able to conceive alternatives, and hence to evaluate. Children need pretend play, in which a banana held to the ear is also a telephone, they need and enjoy this ability to understand that something can be both one thing and something else. This is the basic entry into abstraction, into skills of imagining futures and outcomes, skills of planning. Fiction allows them to enter in imagination many more situations than a lifetime could contain, and in doing so allows them to make mental enactments. This is an essential factor in our development.<br /><br />Second, emotional hygiene. Bears are of course part of the real world and can be seen in some Northern American towns at dusk, picking through the dustbins; however a toy bear will afford an experience of what is called by psychologists attachment, the experience of cuddling and feeling close to a loved one. It is a transitional object that stands in for the for the attachment relationship to a mother or other caregiver. Transitional in the sense that it is transitional between the world of nature - the real attachment to the caregiver - and the world of culture, which includes objects, games, relationships, fiction. A teddy bear models not a real bear with teeth and claws but the relationship of attachment, something intimate and abstract. <br />Fiction is both real and not-real in the same way. It is a game that the child can enter, a miniature version of interactions and emotions. These emotions can be enjoyed safely, and a model of how to manage and resolve such emotions can be constructed. Fear of monsters such as Heffalumps, fear of loss of friendship, fear of being parted from loved ones, all these can become manageable, even enjoyable, in this fictional 'game' context where fear can be explored and resolved.<br /><br />Social empathy: As social animals, an ability to read others' motivations and intentions is crucial to our ability to function in the world. In developmental psychology this is discussed as theory-of-mind or perspective-taking, and mentalizing. Before the age of around four, most children are unable to think that what another person knows is any different from what they know. Tell children this story: Maxi had some chocolate. He put the chocolate into a blue cupboard and then went out to play. While he was out, his mother used some of it to make a cake. Then she put the rest in a different place, in a green cupboard. Then ask the children "When Maxi comes back from the playground he would like some of his chocolate. Where will he look for it?" Children under about four will usually answer "In the green cupboard". They know that is where the chocolate is, and they assume that Maxi will look there. But children of four and over are able to hold in their mind Maxi's perspective, and see that he will look in the blue cupboard, where he left it. <br />There is a strong correlation between narrative skills and theory-of-mind ability. Stories are ideal tools to develop the ability to represent perspective, to accommodate differing perspectives. With increasing maturity we develop the skill not only of making mental models of what we and others know, both now and over longer periods, but also the ability to make models of other people's models, an embedded structure of what people think, feel and believe. Take for example the story of the deception that was carried out on Kanga where Piglet is substituted for Roo. This involves first strategic planning that consists mainly of mentalizing Kanga's reaction to Roo's disappearance, and then in a truly magnificent double bluff, in which Kanga pretends not to notice the difference between the two small animals. The child understands that Kanga knows more than Piglet thinks she knows. There are differing layers of mental models that can and must be accommodated and realised.<br /><br />Lastly creativity. By this I mean more than just the common idea of the ability to come up with a new idea or artefact. I mean more a form of agency in the world, the opposite of compliance to external imposition, rather the sense of being engaged entirely with what we are doing, where the self and the activity become one, where we feel fully alive and absorbed in what we are doing. This has also been called 'flow' - creative living, total involvement in what you are doing. This is what happens when we engage with a work of fiction. In order to do so we need to engage emotionally with the characters, and this is where the Hundred Acre Wood Corporation really shines. Our employees all have distinct issues; for example Pooh is not particularly quick-witted, Piglet struggles with a lack of pluck, Tigger needs more aplomb, Wol could do with a little humility and of course poor Eeyore, well..., anyway all the characters are struggling in such a way that a child can swiftly feel sympathy for them. They become friends. <br />A further requirement for this kind of creative engagement with fiction is that it should not force you to comply with just one view of the world, but should leave gaps and a certain openness, or unexpectedness, space that the imagination has to fill. "In which a house is built at Pooh Corner for Eeyore" can give us a taste of what I mean here: the reader soon realises that Pooh and Piglet have not, in fact, built a new house for Eeyore, but only moved his to the other side of the wood (dramatic irony, the reader knows more than the characters involved). Inevitably, knowing Eeyore's gloomy world view, we feel that disaster and recriminations must ensue. But no, an explanation is found that everyone can live with. <br /><br />BSI inspector: Yes, I can see that you have achieved your targets admirably. But what about your management practices? Can you give me a report on human resource management?<br /><br />100 AW CEO: Well, I admit that we have had issues in the past with in-groups and out-groups, and that these have not been entirely resolved. Efforts were made to integrate Tigger and Kanga and Roo into the in-group around CR, but it must be admitted that there are still some privileges afforded Pooh and Piglet only. However I must say that we are proud of our record on certain aspects of team-building, such as the expotition to find the North Pole, or the management of Owl's house being blown over.<br /><br />BSI inspector: Are you an Equal Opportunity Employer?<br /><br />100 AW CEO: We certainly have a multi-ethnic employee base, with animals from nigh on every continent.<br /><br />BSI inspector: But what about women?<br /><br />100 AW CEO: You must see that the original staff have been with us since a time when it was, let us say, unusual to employ women. Naturally we have considered this question closely. But it is beyond our capacity to engage further staff, so that the only alternative would be to perform a sex-change on a proportion of the characters, something that I think would contravene their rights. And after a thorough survey of our clientele, we found that little girls have no qualms about identifying with male characters, whereas the opposite case, of boys identifying with female characters, is almost unheard-of. Thus we feel that this issue is less important than might be imagined.<br /><br />BSI inspector: Right, well thank you very much. You'll be hearing from us. I still have to visit Hamlet Inc. on this tour.<br /><br /><br />The inspector calls on Hamlet and asks him about terms and definitions of his enterprise:<br /><br />H: Well, yeah, it's a revenge play innit?<br /><br />BSI inspector: What?<br /><br />H: Yeah, y'know, revenge? Hamlet's dad was poisoned by Claudius so now Hamlet has to avenge the old man's death. Lots of blood and gore, a ghost, a bit of pretend madness, the audience just lapped it up. They loved all that stuff. <br /><br />BSI inspector: Yes, but what about your present-day customers? <br /><br />H: It still has a lot to say to kids on the street today. Get out there. Don't stand for no shit. You gotta earn that respect man. No-one's gonna do it for you. They shoot your best boy, you shoot theirs.<br /><br />BSI inspector (turning pale around the nose) Aha. But isn't the language a little, um, difficult for the modern audience? Do you think that something like "Up, sword; and know thou a more horrid hent:" is going to get through to a modern viewer? <br /><br />H: Shit, that's their shit isn't it? I can't go explaining every little thing to them, can I? That's the job of the secondary industry around ours, but you have to inspect them separately, that's not got nuffink to do with me.<br /><br />BSI inspector: What about your employment practices? Do you offer equal opportunities to women, or to ethnic or other minorities?<br /><br />H: Don't make me laugh. All white men, that's what we want. No wops. As for women, well 'Get thee to a nunnery' was the best line ever. Women are no use to anyone. 'Cept for one thing, but I don't get none of that in this piece, which is a shame. <br /><br />(The inspector retreats swiftly)<br /><br />ISO 9000 certificate awarded to Winnie-the-Pooh and friends.<br /><br />(No animals were harmed in the making of this review)<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5064517861515275579" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

We see through the eyes of Pooh who has little attachments in this world besides honey. He is usually the one to come up with solutions to problems and he doesn't overthink things.   <span id="freeText17197717060897433074" style="display:none">I love the purity of this story. It has this child-like wonder about the world. We see through the eyes of Pooh who has little attachments in this world besides honey. He is usually the one to come up with solutions to problems and he doesn't overthink things. He lives by his gut. <br /><br />Each character has such a strong and distinct voice. It's a classic for a reason. You can just hear A. A. Milne telling Christopher Robin these bedtime stories and the boy being delighted. We are just lucky that A. A. decided to write down the wonderful stories and share them with the world.<br /><br />It makes me want to write a children's book. I just love the tone and the voice used here and how things happen, and characters do have minor issues with themselves, but there is overwhelming kindness and compassion in the story and for the community. I read this as an audio book and it was a whole lot of fun to let this story wash over me. It's a quick and easy story to read. There are many many great lines in this book.<br /><br />I need to read the other 3 books of this one. It is simply so special. I'm glad I own this boxset I got on sale and I hope that my niece is going to decide to read this. I never read them as a kid and what a shame. Still, it's a story that I really enjoyed as an adult. Listening, I really felt like the story was similar to a golden light that filters through the leaves of a forest on a happy day and all the world seems to sparkle. There is so much wonder in this book. <br /><br />Pooh Sticks came out of this book. We like to play Pooh sticks on our walks in this area. It's such a Zen kind of game and fun. What a feat this work is and how lucky the world is to have these stories. </span>

<div id="review_166570002" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166570002" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4930332-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577153488p2/4930332.jpg" /></a>

        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/4930332-melanie">Melanie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer166570002" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15151364614163312193">It was with much apprehension that I started this- after all, my initial impression (Winnie the Pooh as it is in Disney) is vastly different from this book. In fact, I don't think I would have even picked up the book, had a friend not recommended it to me. And I'm very glad she did.<br/><br/>I'm surprised by how sad but realistic the end of this book was; with Christopher Robin growing up and everyone realizing he's leaving the Forest, that wonderful place where only one with enough imagination can go. It</span>
  <span id="freeText15151364614163312193" style="display:none">It was with much apprehension that I started this- after all, my initial impression (Winnie the Pooh as it is in Disney) is vastly different from this book. In fact, I don't think I would have even picked up the book, had a friend not recommended it to me. And I'm very glad she did.<br /><br />I'm surprised by how sad but realistic the end of this book was; with Christopher Robin growing up and everyone realizing he's leaving the Forest, that wonderful place where only one with enough imagination can go. It made me cry and revisit my stuffed animal collection that I have abandoned for so long.<br /><br />After rereading both books, I finally figured out why I love them so much. It's because they mirror our lives, in a more simpler way, with people pretending they know everything but really knowing nothing, with others being disillusioned into thinking that certain people do know it all, with others jealous of others' accomplishments, and of course, the beautiful friendships. <br /><br />My favourite part is definitely the one where Rabbit, Pooh, and Piglet trying to make Tigger more humble but ending up with rabbit becoming more humble... that chapter is just the funniest thing I've ever read. Anyways, this collection of stories is just gorgeously told, and ever so funny, yet being a lens of viewing our own world when you strip away the distractions. Definitely five stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15151364614163312193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_235475403" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/235475403" />
    <a title="notgettingenough " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough"><img alt="notgettingenough " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1258115161p2/2658311.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/235475403?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="notgettingenough " class="user" itemprop="url" name="notgettingenough " href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough">notgettingenough </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=sociology">sociology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=science-sort-of">science-sort-of</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=crime-fiction">crime-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=cooking">cooking</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer235475403" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9269903699658084779">For the final of Celebrity Death Match.<br/><br/>Some basic facts about Winnie the Pooh and the Divine Comedy.<br/><br/>(1) Have you ever tried looking up Winnie on project Gutenberg? You find that Dante gets a few thousand hits and Winnie gets none. NONE!!! And you know why? Because Disney bullied Congress years ago into being allowed to keep the copyright longer than was their legal right. And you know why they did that? Of course it is because everybody loves Winnie. Try this, if you don't believe me. Offer the </span>
  <span id="freeText9269903699658084779" style="display:none">For the final of Celebrity Death Match.<br /><br />Some basic facts about Winnie the Pooh and the Divine Comedy.<br /><br />(1) Have you ever tried looking up Winnie on project Gutenberg? You find that Dante gets a few thousand hits and Winnie gets none. NONE!!! And you know why? Because Disney bullied Congress years ago into being allowed to keep the copyright longer than was their legal right. And you know why they did that? Of course it is because everybody loves Winnie. Try this, if you don't believe me. Offer the copyright to The Divine Comedy to Disney for ten bucks.<br /><br />(2) Have you ever tried shopping for Dante sheets? Cursor? Wallpaper - both hard and soft? Mice? Toilet paper? Colouring-in books? Dante stuffed animals? Interactive game sites? <br /><br />(3) google The Divine Comedy and you get 3M hits. google Winnie the Pooh and you get 58M (numbers rounded down, to Dante's advantage).<br /><br />Democracy, ladies and gentlemen. The world has voted. Celebrity death match can scarcely go against figures like these.<br /><br />(4) When I was in Grade three, about seven years old, we were set as English comprehension:<br /><br />"Compare and contrast the following passages"<br /><br />The start of the Divine Comedy:<br /><br />His glory, by whose might all things are mov'd,<br />Pierces the universe, and in one part<br />Sheds more resplendence, elsewhere less. In heav'n,<br />That largeliest of his light partakes, was I,<br />Witness of things, which to relate again<br />Surpasseth power of him who comes from thence;<br />For that, so near approaching its desire<br />Our intellect is to such depth absorb'd,<br />That memory cannot follow. Nathless all,<br />That in my thoughts I of that sacred realm<br />Could store, shall now be matter of my song.<br /><br />and a poem by Pooh:<br /><br />THOUGHTS<br /><br /><br />I lay on my chest<br />And I thought it best<br />To pretend I was having a evening rest;<br />I lay on my tum<br />And I tried to hum<br />But nothing particular seemed to come<br />My face was flat<br />On the floor, and that<br />Is all very well for an acrobat;<br />But it doesn't seem fair<br />To a Friendly Bear<br />To stiffen him out with a backet-chair.<br />And sort of squoze<br />Which grows and grows<br />Is not too nice for his poor old nose,<br />And sort of squch<br />Is much to much<br />For his neck and his mouth and his <br />ears and such.<br /><br /><br />I discussed all the obvious points, the sheer boredom of reading Dante, his inability to call a rhyme. Naturally I compared Pooh favourably with Shakespeare, making the point like others before me, I expect, that they were both inventors of words, that they revelled in the joyous playfullness of language.<br /><br />The coup of my essay, however, was revealing the sociological experiment carried out by my mother. Whilst I was sweetly put to sleep with Pooh each night, my poor brother was served up Dante. He has never recovered from the trauma of it. To him going to bed at night is to be avoided at all costs. Anything but that. And in a truly despicable example of what happens when one is raised on Dante, my mother once found that my brother had hanged his teddy bear.<br /><br />I rest my case.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9269903699658084779" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4132478204691982903" style="display:none">I had a deprived childhood. Ahhh! I did not read any of the Winnie the Pooh stories until I came across this recording &amp; the companion recording of "Winnie the Pooh" until I bought them for my daughter when I was in my 40s. <br /><br />It's the only story which has brought me close to crashing the car. Driving through the Lake District I heard the the Pooh sticks story as Eyeore floats under the bridge. It's difficult to see dry-stone walls, cry laughing uncontrollably, and keep control of a car on tight bends all at the same time. So I had to pull in and get the story finished before carrying on. The motorway signs say "Tiredness can kill" - well so could A A Milne in the voice of Alan Bennett.<br /><br />The humour of the stories is brilliant. The reading is delightful. Bennett's voice and Eyeore's lines were made for each other.<br /> </span>

<div id="review_2990749036" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2990749036" />
    <a title="Abigail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail"><img alt="Abigail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539121384p2/87835420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2990749036?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail">Abigail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780525447740" href="/book/show/2360076.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers Who Enjoy Classic Animal/Toy Fantasy for Children</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=childrens-fiction">childrens-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=childrens-fantasy">childrens-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=animal-fiction">animal-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2990749036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer945337567986622726">A.A. Milne returns to the Hundred Acre Wood in this follow-up to <u>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_' title='Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne' rel='nofollow noopener'>Winnie-the-Pooh</a>
</u>, offering ten charming episodes in which the animals have many adventures together. The marvelous Tigger is introduced here, moving in with Kanga and Roo, getting stuck in a tree, and being deliberately "lost" by Rabbit, in order to debounce him. Quite a few of the characters - Owl, Eeyore, Rabbit - lose their homes, and gain new ones. The book concludes with the melancholy fact that Christopher Robin is going a</span>
  <span id="freeText945337567986622726" style="display:none">A.A. Milne returns to the Hundred Acre Wood in this follow-up to <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_" title="Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie-the-Pooh</a>
</u>, offering ten charming episodes in which the animals have many adventures together. The marvelous Tigger is introduced here, moving in with Kanga and Roo, getting stuck in a tree, and being deliberately "lost" by Rabbit, in order to debounce him. Quite a few of the characters - Owl, Eeyore, Rabbit - lose their homes, and gain new ones. The book concludes with the melancholy fact that Christopher Robin is going away...<br /><br />I honestly have no idea if I read the <i>Winnie the Pooh</i> books as a child. I'm familiar with many of the stories, but I have no clear memory of ever having picked up the books. Perhaps they were read to me, at bedtime? I know that I never saw the Disney film, but I do recall an LP we had growing up, that contained some of the <i>Winnie the Pooh</i> songs from the film. The one about Tigger was my favorite, of course. However that may be, and whenever I was first exposed to Milne's stories, my first actual memory of reading one of them was a few years back, when <u>The House at Pooh Corner</u> was assigned as a text in the course on the history of children's literature I took, while getting my masters. It felt very familiar to me, in a vague sort of way, and was utterly charming. I meant, at that time, to read the other books as well, but time was short, and I didn't end up doing so. In any case, I found this one immensely appealing, appreciated the gentle but compelling stories, the marvelously realized characters, and the sense of sadness at the close. I definitely need to read the entire collection - the two prose volumes (<u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/99107.Winnie_the_Pooh__Winnie_the_Pooh___1_" title="Winnie-the-Pooh (Winnie-the-Pooh, #1) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Winnie-the-Pooh</a>
</u> and <u>The House at Pooh Corner</u>) and the two poetry collections (<u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/821003.When_We_Were_Very_Young__Winnie_the_Pooh___3_" title="When We Were Very Young (Winnie-the-Pooh, #3) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">When We Were Very Young</a>
</u> and <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/821000.Now_We_Are_Six__Winnie_the_Pooh___4_" title="Now We Are Six (Winnie-the-Pooh, #4) by A.A. Milne" rel="nofollow noopener">Now We Are Six</a>
</u>) - at some point.</span>
  <a data-text-id="945337567986622726" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1688091613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1688091613" />
    <a title="Vaishali" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13209822-vaishali"><img alt="Vaishali" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432958582p2/13209822.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1688091613?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vaishali" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vaishali" href="/user/show/13209822-vaishali">Vaishali</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781405211178" href="/book/show/737223.The_House_at_Pooh_Corner">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13209822-vaishali?shelf=european-fiction">european-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1688091613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5345889459893275886">My first time ever with Pooh & friends. Silly, sad, and witty. Some of the smartest quips and soulful reflections I've ever read in children's literature. <3<br/><br/>Quotes:<br/>-------<br/>Owl kept his head and told us that the Opposite of an Introduction… was a Contradiction.<br/><br/>They haven't got Brains, any of them, only grey fluff that's blown into their heads by mistake, and they don't Think…<br/><br/>"What do Jagulars do?" asked Piglet, hoping that they wouldn’t.<br/><br/>… And he was the sort of Tigger who was always in front when </span>
  <span id="freeText5345889459893275886" style="display:none">My first time ever with Pooh &amp; friends. Silly, sad, and witty. Some of the smartest quips and soulful reflections I've ever read in children's literature. &lt;3<br /><br />Quotes:<br />-------<br />Owl kept his head and told us that the Opposite of an Introduction… was a Contradiction.<br /><br />They haven't got Brains, any of them, only grey fluff that's blown into their heads by mistake, and they don't Think…<br /><br />"What do Jagulars do?" asked Piglet, hoping that they wouldn’t.<br /><br />… And he was the sort of Tigger who was always in front when you were showing him the way anywhere, and was generally out of sight when at last you came to the place and said proudly "Here we are!”<br /><br />… You can't help respecting anybody who can spell TUESDAY, even if he doesn't spell it right; but spelling isn't everything. There are days when spelling Tuesday simply doesn't count.<br /><br />“Owl," said Rabbit shortly, "you and I have brains. The others have fluff. If there is any thinking to be done in this Forest -- and when I say thinking I mean thinking -- you and I must do it.”<br /><br />Owl looked at him, and wondered whether to push him off the tree; but, feeling that he could always do it afterwards, he tried once more to find out what they were talking about.<br /><br />"Well, I sort of made it up," said Pooh. “… it comes to me sometimes."<br />"Ah!" said Rabbit, who never let things come to him, but always went and fetched them.<br /><br />“… The stream had grown up… and, being grown-up, it did not run and jump and sparkle along as it used to do when it was younger, but moved more slowly. For it knew now where it was going, and it said to itself, "There is no hurry. We shall get there someday." But all the little streams higher up in the Forest went this way and that, quickly, eagerly, having so much to find out before it was too late.”<br /><br />… When you are a Bear of Very Little Brain, and you Think of Things, you find sometimes that a Thing which seemed very Thingish inside you is quite different when it gets out into the open and has other people looking at it.<br /><br />Pooh, who felt more and more that he was somewhere else, got up slowly and began to look for himself.<br /><br />Roo… went into a corner and practiced jumping… partly because he wanted to practice this, and partly because he didn't want Christopher Robin and Tigger to think that he minded when they went off without him.<br /><br />Said Piglet, "Rabbit's clever…”<br />There was a long silence.<br />"I suppose," said Pooh, “…that’s why he never understands anything."<br /><br />Owl explained about the Necessary Dorsal Muscles. He had explained this to Pooh and Christopher Robin once before, and had been waiting ever since for a chance to do it again, because it is a thing which you can easily explain twice before anybody knows what you are talking about.<br /><br />Everybody said "How-do-you-do" to Eeyore, and Eeyore said that he didn’t…<br /><br />"Where are we going?” said Pooh, hurrying after him, and wondering whether it was to be an Explore or a What-shall-I-do-about-you-know-what.<br />"Nowhere," said Christopher Robin.<br />So they began going there…<br /><br />…They came to an enchanted place on the very top of the Forest... which is sixty-something trees in a circle; and Christopher Robin knew that it was enchanted because nobody had ever been able to count whether it was sixty-three or sixty-four…<br /><br />“Sitting there they could see the whole world spread out until it reached the sky, and whatever there was all the world over was with them...<br /><br />"Pooh, promise you won't forget about me, ever. Not ever…"<br />"I promise," he said.<br /><br />So they went off together. But wherever they go, and whatever happens to them on the way, in that enchanted place on the top of the Forest a little boy and his Bear will always be playing.<br /><br /><br />&lt;3 &lt;3 &lt;3</span>
  <a data-text-id="5345889459893275886" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1625379100" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1625379100" />
    <a title="Amanda G. Stevens" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9779665-amanda-g-stevens"><img alt="Amanda G. Stevens" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442981901p2/9779665.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1625379100?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda G. Stevens" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda G. Stevens" href="/user/show/9779665-amanda-g-stevens">Amanda G. Stevens</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1625379100" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3859476717418140029">Gentle and sweet and sprinkled with unexpected wisdom (or should I say, wisdom from unexpected sources). I grew up on the animated <i>New Adventures of Winnie-the-Pooh</i>, but back then I (of course) didn't see how the characters fit definite personality types; that was fun to track while reading Milne for the first time. Also the layers of wordplay and wit are delightful. All in all, I was going to give this a strong four stars until the last story, at which point I cried for Christopher Robin and Po</span>
  <span id="freeText3859476717418140029" style="display:none">Gentle and sweet and sprinkled with unexpected wisdom (or should I say, wisdom from unexpected sources). I grew up on the animated <i>New Adventures of Winnie-the-Pooh</i>, but back then I (of course) didn't see how the characters fit definite personality types; that was fun to track while reading Milne for the first time. Also the layers of wordplay and wit are delightful. All in all, I was going to give this a strong four stars until the last story, at which point I cried for Christopher Robin and Pooh and every child who knows it's time to start growing up yet doesn't want to or feel ready for it. As a kid, I felt that tearing too, that loss of putting away childish things. This is a beautiful book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3859476717418140029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6876539374316845496">I never had read the orginal Pooh books, so decided I should.<br/><br/>To quote from the back cover:<br/>"Private research has convinced us that few people in responsible positions in society today have got to their eminence without the influence of Pooh"<br/>- The Christian Science Monitor<br/><br/>And The House at Pooh Corner is home to one of my most favorite Pooh quotes (which I collected from somewhere other than the book, I guess):<br/>"Piglet sidled up to Pooh from behind.<br/>'Pooh!' he whispered.<br/>'Yes, piglet?'<br/>'Nothing,' said </span>
  <span id="freeText6876539374316845496" style="display:none">I never had read the orginal Pooh books, so decided I should.<br /><br />To quote from the back cover:<br />"Private research has convinced us that few people in responsible positions in society today have got to their eminence without the influence of Pooh"<br />- The Christian Science Monitor<br /><br />And The House at Pooh Corner is home to one of my most favorite Pooh quotes (which I collected from somewhere other than the book, I guess):<br />"Piglet sidled up to Pooh from behind.<br />'Pooh!' he whispered.<br />'Yes, piglet?'<br />'Nothing,' said Piglet, taking Pooh's paw. 'I just wanted to be sure of you.'"<br /><br />Love it. :)</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9u6CCPLiRJZeG3asXG4Lp+wRfenLwGKHbm+RPX1cMffMyOvaSbyN/vkwws2rmwF4Ok6aIQVN7OA1EdeMsB/DbQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('bDn/Eb0jSrPhNqJf4+7Ya7wmNDd3gRk1DfkeSZ6bAn9WH5bDBn2D20YdFj4UG9K0annT/7kMl1JWh1j4U9jw5Q==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/81466.A_A_Milne">About A.A. Milne</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/81466.A_A_Milne"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1204664899p3/81466.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/81466.A_A_Milne">A.A. Milne
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11084563760391224683">Alan Alexander Milne (pronounced /ˈmɪln/) was an English author, best known for his books about the teddy bear Winnie-the-Pooh and for various children's poems. <br/><br/>A. A. Milne was born in Kilburn, London, to parents Vince Milne and Sarah Marie Milne (née Heginbotham) and grew up at Henley House School, 6/7 Mortimer Road (now Crescent), Kilburn, a small public school run by his father. One of his teac</span>
  <span id="freeText11084563760391224683" style="display:none">Alan Alexander Milne (pronounced /ˈmɪln/) was an English author, best known for his books about the teddy bear Winnie-the-Pooh and for various children's poems. <br /><br />A. A. Milne was born in Kilburn, London, to parents Vince Milne and Sarah Marie Milne (née Heginbotham) and grew up at Henley House School, 6/7 Mortimer Road (now Crescent), Kilburn, a small public school run by his father. One of his teachers was H. G. Wells who taught there in 1889–90. Milne attended Westminster School and Trinity College, Cambridge, where he studied on a mathematics scholarship. While there, he edited and wrote for Granta, a student magazine. He collaborated with his brother Kenneth and their articles appeared over the initials AKM. Milne's work came to the attention of the leading British humour magazine Punch, where Milne was to become a contributor and later an assistant editor.<br /> <br />Milne joined the British Army in World War I and served as an officer in the Royal Warwickshire Regiment and later, after a debilitating illness, the Royal Corps of Signals. He was discharged on February 14, 1919.<br /> <br />After the war, he wrote a denunciation of war titled Peace with Honour (1934), which he retracted somewhat with 1940's War with Honour. During World War II, Milne was one of the most prominent critics of English writer P. G. Wodehouse, who was captured at his country home in France by the Nazis and imprisoned for a year. Wodehouse made radio broadcasts about his internment, which were broadcast from Berlin. Although the light-hearted broadcasts made fun of the Germans, Milne accused Wodehouse of committing an act of near treason by cooperating with his country's enemy. Wodehouse got some revenge on his former friend by creating fatuous parodies of the Christopher Robin poems in some of his later stories, and claiming that Milne "was probably jealous of all other writers.... But I loved his stuff."<br /> <br />He married Dorothy "Daphne" de Sélincourt in 1913, and their only son, Christopher Robin Milne, was born in 1920. In 1925, A. A. Milne bought a country home, Cotchford Farm, in Hartfield, East Sussex. During World War II, A. A. Milne was Captain of the Home Guard in Hartfield &amp; Forest Row, insisting on being plain 'Mr. Milne' to the members of his platoon. He retired to the farm after a stroke and brain surgery in 1952 left him an invalid and by August 1953 "he seemed very old and disenchanted".<br /><br />He was 74 years old when he passed away in 1956.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11084563760391224683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
